<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Property that can influence motion</div>

title="Category:Classical mechanics">Category</a></span></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Classical_mechanics" title="Template:Classical mechanics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Classical_mechanics" title="Template talk:Classical mechanics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Classical_mechanics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, a <b>force</b> is an influence that can change the <a href="/wiki/Motion" title="Motion">motion</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Physical_object" title="Physical object">object</a>. A force can cause an object with <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> to change its <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> (e.g. moving from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_first_law" title="Newton's first law">state of rest</a>), i.e., to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerate" title="Accelerate">accelerate</a>. Force can also be described intuitively as a push or a pull. A force has both <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector#Length" title="Euclidean vector">magnitude</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direction_(geometry,_geography)" title="Direction (geometry, geography)">direction</a>, making it a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(geometric)" title="Vector (geometric)">vector</a> quantity.  It is measured in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_unit" title="SI unit">SI unit</a> of <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">newton (N)</a>. Force is represented by the symbol <span class="texhtml"><b>F</b></span> (formerly <span class="texhtml"><b>P</b></span>).
</p><p>The original form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_second_law" title="Newton's second law">Newton's second law</a> states that the net force acting upon an object is equal to the <a href="/wiki/Time_derivative" title="Time derivative">rate</a> at which its <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> changes with <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>. If the mass of the object is constant, this law implies that the <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> of an object is directly <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">proportional</a> to the net force acting on the object, is in the direction of the net force, and is inversely proportional to the <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> of the object.
</p><p>Concepts related to force include: <a href="/wiki/Thrust" title="Thrust">thrust</a>, which increases the velocity of an object; <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a>, which decreases the velocity of an object; and <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a>, which produces <a href="/wiki/Angular_acceleration" title="Angular acceleration">changes in rotational speed</a> of an object. In an extended body, each part usually applies forces on the adjacent parts; the distribution of such forces through the body is the internal <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">mechanical stress</a>. Such internal mechanical stresses cause no acceleration of that body as the forces balance one another. <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">Pressure</a>, the distribution of many small forces applied over an area of a body, is a simple type of stress that if unbalanced can cause the body to accelerate. Stress usually causes <a href="/wiki/Deformation_(engineering)" title="Deformation (engineering)">deformation</a> of solid materials, or flow in <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluids</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_the_concept">Development of the concept</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Development of the concept">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Philosophers in <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">antiquity</a> used the concept of force in the study of <a href="/wiki/Statics" title="Statics">stationary</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynamics_(physics)" title="Dynamics (physics)">moving</a> objects and <a href="/wiki/Simple_machine" title="Simple machine">simple machines</a>, but thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> retained fundamental errors in understanding force. In part this was due to an incomplete understanding of the sometimes non-obvious force of <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a>, and a consequently inadequate view of the nature of natural motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archimedes_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Archimedes-1">[1]</a></sup> A fundamental error was the belief that a force is required to maintain motion, even at a constant velocity. Most of the previous misunderstandings about motion and force were eventually corrected by <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Isaac_Newton" title="Sir Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a>. With his mathematical insight, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Isaac_Newton" title="Sir Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a> formulated <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">laws of motion</a> that were not improved for nearly three hundred years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_ch2_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-uniphysics_ch2-2">[2]</a></sup> By the early 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a> developed a <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">theory of relativity</a> that correctly predicted the action of forces on objects with increasing momenta near the speed of light, and also provided insight into the forces produced by gravitation and <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a>.
</p><p>With modern insights into <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> and technology that can accelerate particles close to the speed of light, <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> has devised a <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> to describe forces between particles smaller than atoms. The <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> predicts that exchanged particles called <a href="/wiki/Gauge_boson" title="Gauge boson">gauge bosons</a> are the fundamental means by which forces are emitted and absorbed. Only four main interactions are known: in order of decreasing strength, they are: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_force" title="Strong force">strong</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_force" title="Weak force">weak</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_force" title="Gravitational force">gravitational</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2–10">: 2–10 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 79">: 79 </span></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_energy_physics" title="High energy physics">High-energy particle physics</a> <a href="/wiki/Observation" title="Observation">observations</a> made during the 1970s and 1980s confirmed that the weak and electromagnetic forces are expressions of a more fundamental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electroweak" title="Electroweak">electroweak</a> interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-final_theory_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-final_theory-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Newtonian_concepts">Pre-Newtonian concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-Newtonian concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">Aristotelian physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_impetus" title="Theory of impetus">Theory of impetus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristoteles_Louvre2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="752" data-file-width="536" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Aristoteles_Louvre2.jpg/170px-Aristoteles_Louvre2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Aristoteles_Louvre2.jpg/255px-Aristoteles_Louvre2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Aristoteles_Louvre2.jpg/340px-Aristoteles_Louvre2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristoteles_Louvre2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> famously described a force as anything that causes an object to undergo "unnatural motion"</div></div></div>
<p>Since antiquity the concept of force has been recognized as integral to the functioning of each of the <a href="/wiki/Simple_machine" title="Simple machine">simple machines</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_advantage" title="Mechanical advantage">mechanical advantage</a> given by a simple machine allowed for less force to be used in exchange for that force acting over a greater distance for the same amount of <a href="/wiki/Work_(physics)" title="Work (physics)">work</a>. Analysis of the characteristics of forces ultimately culminated in the work of <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> who was especially famous for formulating a treatment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buoyant_force" title="Buoyant force">buoyant forces</a> inherent in <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archimedes_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Archimedes-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> provided a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophical" title="Philosophical">philosophical</a> discussion of the concept of a force as an integral part of <a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">Aristotelian cosmology</a>. In Aristotle's view, the terrestrial sphere contained four <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">elements</a> that come to rest at different "natural places" therein. Aristotle believed that motionless objects on Earth, those composed mostly of the elements earth and water, to be in their natural place on the ground and that they will stay that way if left alone. He distinguished between the innate tendency of objects to find their "natural place" (e.g., for heavy bodies to fall), which led to "natural motion", and unnatural or forced motion, which required continued application of a force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> This theory, based on the everyday experience of how objects move, such as the constant application of a force needed to keep a cart moving, had conceptual trouble accounting for the behavior of <a href="/wiki/Projectile" title="Projectile">projectiles</a>, such as the flight of arrows. The place where the archer moves the projectile was at the start of the flight, and while the projectile sailed through the air, no discernible efficient cause acts on it. Aristotle was aware of this problem and proposed that the air displaced through the projectile's path carries the projectile to its target. This explanation demands a continuum like air for change of place in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hetherington_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hetherington-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">Aristotelian physics</a> began facing criticism in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Science in the Middle Ages">medieval science</a>, first by <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a> in the 6th century.
</p><p>The shortcomings of Aristotelian physics would not be fully corrected until the 17th century work of <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, who was influenced by the late medieval idea that objects in forced motion carried an innate force of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impetus_theory" title="Impetus theory">impetus</a>. Galileo constructed an experiment in which stones and cannonballs were both rolled down an incline to disprove the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotelian_theory_of_gravity" title="Aristotelian theory of gravity">Aristotelian theory of motion</a>. He showed that the bodies were accelerated by gravity to an extent that was independent of their mass and argued that objects retain their <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> unless acted on by a force, for example <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galileo_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galileo-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 17th century, before Newton's Principia, the term "force" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">vis</i>) was applied to many physical and non-physical phenomena, e.g., for an acceleration of a point. The product of a point mass and the square of its velocity was named <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">vis viva</i></span> (live force) by <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a>. The modern concept of force corresponds to Newton's <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">vis motrix</i></span> (accelerating force).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Newtonian_mechanics">Newtonian mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Newtonian mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's laws of motion</a></div>
<p>Sir Isaac Newton described the motion of all objects using the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a> and force, and in doing so he found they obey certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_laws" title="Conservation laws">conservation laws</a>. In 1687, Newton published his thesis <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_ch2_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-uniphysics_ch2-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Principia_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Principia-10">[10]</a></sup> In this work Newton set out three laws of motion that to this day are the way forces are described in physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Principia_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Principia-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_law">First law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: First law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_first_law" title="Newton's first law">Newton's first law</a></div>
<p>Newton's first law of motion states that objects continue to move in a state of constant velocity unless acted upon by an external <a href="/wiki/Net_force" title="Net force">net force</a> (resultant force).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Principia_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Principia-10">[10]</a></sup> This law is an extension of Galileo's insight that constant velocity was associated with a lack of net force (see <a href="#Dynamic_equilibrium">a more detailed description of this below</a>). Newton proposed that every object with mass has an innate <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a> that functions as the fundamental equilibrium "natural state" in place of the Aristotelian idea of the "natural state of rest". That is, Newton's empirical first law contradicts the intuitive Aristotelian belief that a net force is required to keep an object moving with constant velocity. By making <i>rest</i> physically indistinguishable from <i>non-zero constant velocity</i>, Newton's first law directly connects inertia with the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_relativity" title="Galilean relativity">relative velocities</a>. Specifically, in systems where objects are moving with different velocities, it is impossible to determine which object is "in motion" and which object is "at rest". The laws of physics are the same in every <a href="/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference" title="Inertial frame of reference">inertial frame of reference</a>, that is, in all frames related by a <a href="/wiki/Galilean_transformation" title="Galilean transformation">Galilean transformation</a>.
</p><p>For instance, while traveling in a moving vehicle at a constant <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a>, the laws of physics do not change as a result of its motion. If a person riding within the vehicle throws a ball straight up, that person will observe it rise vertically and fall vertically and not have to apply a force in the direction the vehicle is moving. Another person, observing the moving vehicle pass by, would observe the ball follow a curving <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabolic path</a> in the same direction as the motion of the vehicle. It is the inertia of the ball associated with its constant velocity in the direction of the vehicle's motion that ensures the ball continues to move forward even as it is thrown up and falls back down. From the perspective of the person in the car, the vehicle and everything inside of it is at rest: It is the outside world that is moving with a constant speed in the opposite direction of the vehicle. Since there is no experiment that can distinguish whether it is the vehicle that is at rest or the outside world that is at rest, the two situations are considered to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_equivalence" title="Galilean equivalence">physically indistinguishable</a>. Inertia therefore applies equally well to constant velocity motion as it does to rest.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1916" data-file-width="1364" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/170px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/255px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/340px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Though <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Isaac_Newton" title="Sir Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a>'s most famous equation is<br/> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=m{\vec {a}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_law">Second law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Second law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\vec {v}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/85820588abd7333ef4d0c56539cb31c20e730753" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.175ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9–1, 9–2">: 9–1, 9–2 </span></sup>
</p><p>If Newton's second law is applied to a system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_Laws_of_Motion#Open_systems" title="Newton's Laws of Motion">constant mass</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halliday_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halliday-12">[Note 2]</a></sup> <i>m</i> may be moved outside the derivative operator. The equation then becomes
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=m{\frac {\mathrm {d} {\vec {v}}}{\mathrm {d} t}}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi>
</mrow>
<mi>t</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=m{\frac {\mathrm {d} {\vec {v}}}{\mathrm {d} t}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=m{\frac {\mathrm {d} {\vec {v}}}{\mathrm {d} t}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fbb33f421e7833ee7a3e641d7ea267d837d6ae8d" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:10.861ex; height:5.509ex;"/></div>
By substituting the definition of <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a>, the algebraic version of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_second_law" title="Newton's second law">Newton's second law</a> is derived:
<div class="mwe-math-element" data-qid="Q2397319"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Special:MathWikibase&amp;qid=Q2397319" style="color:inherit;"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=m{\vec {a}}.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>F</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>a</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=m{\vec {a}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=m{\vec {a}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7a0dadf563d53093ce358efde4df581e8f586bf3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.786ex; height:2.843ex;"/></a></div>
Newton never explicitly stated the formula in the reduced form above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Newton's second law asserts the direct proportionality of acceleration to force and the inverse proportionality of acceleration to mass. Accelerations can be defined through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinematic" title="Kinematic">kinematic</a> measurements. However, while kinematics are well-described through <a href="/wiki/Frame_of_reference" title="Frame of reference">reference frame</a> analysis in advanced physics, there are still deep questions that remain as to what is the proper definition of mass. <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">General relativity</a> offers an equivalence between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space-time" title="Space-time">space-time</a> and mass, but lacking a coherent theory of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a>, it is unclear as to how or whether this connection is relevant on microscales. With some justification, Newton's second law can be taken as a quantitative definition of <i>mass</i> by writing the law as an equality; the relative units of force and mass then are fixed.
</p><p>Some textbooks use Newton's second law as a <i>definition</i> of force,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup> but this has been disparaged in other textbooks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 12–1">: 12–1 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 59">: 59 </span></sup> Notable physicists, philosophers and mathematicians who have sought a more explicit definition of the concept of force include <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mach" title="Ernst Mach">Ernst Mach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Noll" title="Walter Noll">Walter Noll</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Newton's second law can be used to measure the strength of forces. For instance, knowledge of the masses of <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> along with the accelerations of their <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a> allows scientists to calculate the gravitational forces on planets.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_law">Third law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Third law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_third_law" title="Newton's third law">Newton's third law</a></div>
<p>Whenever one body exerts a force on another, the latter simultaneously exerts an equal and opposite force on the first. In vector form, if <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}_{1,2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>

This law is sometimes referred to as the <i>action-reaction law</i>, with <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}_{1,2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>Newton's Third Law is a result of applying <a href="/wiki/Symmetry" title="Symmetry">symmetry</a> to situations where forces can be attributed to the presence of different objects. The third law means that all forces are <i>interactions</i> between different bodies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[Note 3]</a></sup> and thus that there is no such thing as a unidirectional force or a force that acts on only one body.
</p><p>In a system composed of object 1 and object 2, the net force on the system due to their mutual interactions is zero:

More generally, in a <a href="/wiki/Closed_system" title="Closed system">closed system</a> of particles, all internal forces are balanced. The particles may accelerate with respect to each other but the <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> of the system will not accelerate. If an external force acts on the system, it will make the center of mass accelerate in proportion to the magnitude of the external force divided by the mass of the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 19–1">: 19–1 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Combining Newton's Second and Third Laws, it is possible to show that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_momentum" title="Conservation of momentum">linear momentum of a system is conserved</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> In a system of two particles, if <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {p}}_{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<msub>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {\mathrm {d} {\vec {p}}_{1}}{\mathrm {d} t}}+{\frac {\mathrm {d} {\vec {p}}_{2}}{\mathrm {d} t}}={\vec {F}}_{1,2}+{\vec {F}}_{2,1}=0.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\frac {\mathrm {d} {\vec {p}}_{1}}{\mathrm {d} t}}+{\frac {\mathrm {d} {\vec {p}}_{2}}{\mathrm {d} t}}={\vec {F}}_{1,2}+{\vec {F}}_{2,1}=0.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a52b118d12359de8ed667eed83fcb3dc1fb14be9" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:30.912ex; height:5.676ex;"/></div>
Using similar arguments, this can be generalized to a system with an arbitrary number of particles. In general, as long as all forces are due to the interaction of objects with mass, it is possible to define a system such that net momentum is never lost nor gained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_theory_of_relativity">Special theory of relativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Special theory of relativity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_theory_of_relativity" title="Special theory of relativity">special theory of relativity</a>, mass and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> are equivalent (as can be seen by calculating the work required to accelerate an object). When an object's velocity increases, so does its energy and hence its mass equivalent (inertia). It thus requires more force to accelerate it the same amount than it did at a lower velocity. Newton's Second Law

Relativistic force does not produce a constant acceleration, but an ever-decreasing acceleration as the object approaches the speed of light. Note that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \gamma }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="A^{\mu }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e1da55ba40017c25d210f7e269efb2d6d539a1b6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.967ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Four-acceleration" title="Four-acceleration">four-acceleration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Descriptions">Descriptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Descriptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Freebodydiagram3_pn.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Freebodydiagram3_pn.svg/220px-Freebodydiagram3_pn.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Freebodydiagram3_pn.svg/330px-Freebodydiagram3_pn.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Freebodydiagram3_pn.svg/440px-Freebodydiagram3_pn.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Freebodydiagram3_pn.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Free_body_diagram" title="Free body diagram">Free body diagrams</a> of a block on a flat surface and an <a href="/wiki/Inclined_plane" title="Inclined plane">inclined plane</a>. Forces are resolved and added together to determine their magnitudes and the net force.</div></div></div>
<p>Since forces are perceived as pushes or pulls, this can provide an intuitive understanding for describing forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_ch2_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-uniphysics_ch2-2">[2]</a></sup> As with other physical concepts (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>), the intuitive understanding of forces is quantified using precise <a href="/wiki/Operational_definition" title="Operational definition">operational definitions</a> that are consistent with direct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_perception" title="Sensory perception">observations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">compared to a standard measurement scale</a>. Through experimentation, it is determined that laboratory measurements of forces are fully consistent with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conceptual_definition" title="Conceptual definition">conceptual definition</a> of force offered by <a href="#Newtonian_mechanics">Newtonian mechanics</a>.
</p><p>Forces act in a particular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direction_(geometry)" title="Direction (geometry)">direction</a> and have <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude (mathematics)">sizes</a> dependent upon how strong the push or pull is. Because of these characteristics, forces are classified as "<a href="/wiki/Euclidean_vector" title="Euclidean vector">vector quantities</a>". This means that forces follow a different set of mathematical rules than physical quantities that do not have direction (denoted <a href="/wiki/Scalar_(physics)" title="Scalar (physics)">scalar</a> quantities). For example, when determining what happens when two forces act on the same object, it is necessary to know both the magnitude and the direction of both forces to calculate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resultant_vector" title="Resultant vector">result</a>. If both of these pieces of information are not known for each force, the situation is ambiguous. For example, if you know that two people are pulling on the same rope with known magnitudes of force but you do not know which direction either person is pulling, it is impossible to determine what the acceleration of the rope will be. The two people could be pulling against each other as in <a href="/wiki/Tug_of_war" title="Tug of war">tug of war</a> or the two people could be pulling in the same direction. In this simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One-dimensional" title="One-dimensional">one-dimensional</a> example, without knowing the direction of the forces it is impossible to decide whether the net force is the result of adding the two force magnitudes or subtracting one from the other. Associating forces with vectors avoids such problems.
</p><p>Historically, forces were first quantitatively investigated in conditions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Static_equilibrium" title="Static equilibrium">static equilibrium</a> where several forces canceled each other out. Such experiments demonstrate the crucial properties that forces are additive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(geometric)" title="Vector (geometric)">vector quantities</a>: they have <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude (mathematics)">magnitude</a> and direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_ch2_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-uniphysics_ch2-2">[2]</a></sup> When two forces act on a <a href="/wiki/Point_particle" title="Point particle">point particle</a>, the resulting force, the <i>resultant</i> (also called the <i><a href="/wiki/Net_force" title="Net force">net force</a></i>), can be determined by following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parallelogram_rule" title="Parallelogram rule">parallelogram rule</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_addition" title="Vector addition">vector addition</a>: the addition of two vectors represented by sides of a parallelogram, gives an equivalent resultant vector that is equal in magnitude and direction to the transversal of the parallelogram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup> The magnitude of the resultant varies from the difference of the magnitudes of the two forces to their sum, depending on the angle between their lines of action. However, if the forces are acting on an extended body, their respective lines of application must also be specified in order to account for their effects on the motion of the body.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free-body_diagram" title="Free-body diagram">Free-body diagrams</a> can be used as a convenient way to keep track of forces acting on a system. Ideally, these diagrams are drawn with the angles and relative magnitudes of the force vectors preserved so that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(geometric)" title="Vector (geometric)">graphical vector addition</a> can be done to determine the net force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>As well as being added, forces can also be resolved into independent components at <a href="/wiki/Right_angle" title="Right angle">right angles</a> to each other. A horizontal force pointing northeast can therefore be split into two forces, one pointing north, and one pointing east. Summing these component forces using vector addition yields the original force. Resolving force vectors into components of a set of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basis_vector" title="Basis vector">basis vectors</a> is often a more mathematically clean way to describe forces than using magnitudes and directions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup> This is because, for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthogonal" title="Orthogonal">orthogonal</a> components, the components of the vector sum are uniquely determined by the scalar addition of the components of the individual vectors. Orthogonal components are independent of each other because forces acting at ninety degrees to each other have no effect on the magnitude or direction of the other. Choosing a set of orthogonal basis vectors is often done by considering what set of basis vectors will make the mathematics most convenient. Choosing a basis vector that is in the same direction as one of the forces is desirable, since that force would then have only one non-zero component. Orthogonal force vectors can be three-dimensional with the third component being at right-angles to the other two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equilibrium">Equilibrium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Equilibrium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When all the forces that act upon an object are balanced, then the object is said to be in a state of <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_equilibrium" title="Mechanical equilibrium">equilibrium</a>. Hence, equilibrium occurs when the resultant force acting on a point particle is zero (that is, the vector sum of all forces is zero). When dealing with an extended body, it is also necessary that the net torque be zero.
</p><p>There are two kinds of equilibrium: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Static_equilibrium" title="Static equilibrium">static equilibrium</a> and <a href="#Dynamic_equilibrium">dynamic equilibrium</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Static"><span class="anchor" id="Static_equilibrium"></span>Static</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Static">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Statics" title="Statics">Statics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Static_equilibrium" title="Static equilibrium">Static equilibrium</a></div>
<p>Static equilibrium was understood well before the invention of classical mechanics. Objects that are at rest have zero net force acting on them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The simplest case of static equilibrium occurs when two forces are equal in magnitude but opposite in direction. For example, an object on a level surface is pulled (attracted) downward toward the center of the Earth by the force of gravity. At the same time, a force is applied by the surface that resists the downward force with equal upward force (called a <a href="/wiki/Normal_force" title="Normal force">normal force</a>). The situation produces zero net force and hence no acceleration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_ch2_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-uniphysics_ch2-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pushing against an object that rests on a frictional surface can result in a situation where the object does not move because the applied force is opposed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Static_friction" title="Static friction">static friction</a>, generated between the object and the table surface. For a situation with no movement, the static friction force <i>exactly</i> balances the applied force resulting in no acceleration. The static friction increases or decreases in response to the applied force up to an upper limit determined by the characteristics of the contact between the surface and the object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_ch2_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-uniphysics_ch2-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>A static equilibrium between two forces is the most usual way of measuring forces, using simple devices such as <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale" title="Weighing scale">weighing scales</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_balance" title="Spring balance">spring balances</a>. For example, an object suspended on a vertical <a href="/wiki/Spring_scale" title="Spring scale">spring scale</a> experiences the force of gravity acting on the object balanced by a force applied by the "spring reaction force", which equals the object's weight. Using such tools, some quantitative force laws were discovered: that the force of gravity is proportional to volume for objects of constant <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> (widely exploited for millennia to define standard weights); <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_principle" title="Archimedes' principle">Archimedes' principle</a> for buoyancy; Archimedes' analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Lever" title="Lever">lever</a>; <a href="/wiki/Boyle%27s_law" title="Boyle's law">Boyle's law</a> for gas pressure; and <a href="/wiki/Hooke%27s_law" title="Hooke's law">Hooke's law</a> for springs. These were all formulated and experimentally verified before Isaac Newton expounded his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_Laws_of_Motion" title="Newton's Laws of Motion">Three Laws of Motion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_ch2_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-uniphysics_ch2-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dynamic"><span class="anchor" id="Dynamical_equilibrium"></span><span class="anchor" id="Dynamic_equilibrium"></span>Dynamic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Dynamic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynamics_(physics)" title="Dynamics (physics)">Dynamics (physics)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1448" data-file-width="1180" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/170px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/255px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/340px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> was the first to point out the inherent contradictions contained in Aristotle's description of forces.</div></div></div>
<p>Dynamic equilibrium was first described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a> who noticed that certain assumptions of Aristotelian physics were contradicted by observations and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>. Galileo realized that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_relativity" title="Galilean relativity">simple velocity addition</a> demands that the concept of an "absolute <a href="/wiki/Rest_frame" title="Rest frame">rest frame</a>" did not exist. Galileo concluded that motion in a constant <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> was completely equivalent to rest. This was contrary to Aristotle's notion of a "natural state" of rest that objects with mass naturally approached. Simple experiments showed that Galileo's understanding of the equivalence of constant velocity and rest were correct. For example, if a mariner dropped a cannonball from the crow's nest of a ship moving at a constant velocity, Aristotelian physics would have the cannonball fall straight down while the ship moved beneath it. Thus, in an Aristotelian universe, the falling cannonball would land behind the foot of the mast of a moving ship. However, when this experiment is actually conducted, the cannonball always falls at the foot of the mast, as if the cannonball knows to travel with the ship despite being separated from it. Since there is no forward horizontal force being applied on the cannonball as it falls, the only conclusion left is that the cannonball continues to move with the same velocity as the boat as it falls. Thus, no force is required to keep the cannonball moving at the constant forward velocity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galileo_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Galileo-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Moreover, any object traveling at a constant velocity must be subject to zero net force (resultant force). This is the definition of dynamic equilibrium: when all the forces on an object balance but it still moves at a constant velocity.
</p><p>A simple case of dynamic equilibrium occurs in constant velocity motion across a surface with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinetic_friction" title="Kinetic friction">kinetic friction</a>. In such a situation, a force is applied in the direction of motion while the kinetic friction force exactly opposes the applied force. This results in zero net force, but since the object started with a non-zero velocity, it continues to move with a non-zero velocity. Aristotle misinterpreted this motion as being caused by the applied force. However, when kinetic friction is taken into consideration it is clear that there is no net force causing constant velocity motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forces_in_quantum_mechanics">Forces in quantum mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Forces in quantum mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">Quantum mechanics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a></div>
<p>The notion "force" keeps its meaning in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, though one is now dealing with operators  instead of classical variables and though the physics is now described by the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a> instead of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtonian_mechanics" title="Newtonian mechanics">Newtonian equations</a>. This has the consequence that the results of a measurement are now sometimes "quantized", i.e. they appear in discrete portions. This is, of course, difficult to imagine in the context of "forces". However, the potentials <span class="texhtml"><i>V</i>(<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, <i>z</i>)</span> or <a href="/wiki/Field_(physics)" title="Field (physics)">fields</a>, from which the forces generally can be derived, are treated similarly to classical position variables, i.e., <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle V(x,y,z)\to {\hat {V}}({\hat {x}},{\hat {y}},{\hat {z}})}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>V</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>y</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>z</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle V(x,y,z)\to {\hat {V}}({\hat {x}},{\hat {y}},{\hat {z}})}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="V(x,y,z)\to {\hat {V}}({\hat {x}},{\hat {y}},{\hat {z}})" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/505aeeefb4e05b4ccec6873172de7c6efe014ed3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:22.444ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span>.
</p><p>This becomes different only in the framework of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a>, where these fields are also quantized.
</p><p>However, already in quantum mechanics there is one "caveat", namely the particles acting onto each other do not only possess the spatial variable, but also a discrete  intrinsic <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum-like</a> variable called the "<a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a>", and there is the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a> relating the space and the spin variables. Depending on the value of the spin, identical particles split into two different classes, <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boson" title="Boson">bosons</a>.  If two identical fermions (e.g. electrons) have a <i>symmetric</i> spin function (e.g.  parallel spins) the spatial variables must be <i>antisymmetric</i> (i.e. they exclude each other from their places much as if there was a repulsive force), and vice versa, i.e. for antiparallel <i>spins</i> the <i>position variables</i> must be symmetric (i.e. the apparent force must be attractive). Thus in the case of two fermions there is a strictly negative correlation between spatial and spin variables, whereas for two bosons (e.g. quanta of electromagnetic waves, photons) the correlation is strictly positive.
</p><p>Thus the notion "force" loses already part of its meaning.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feynman_diagrams">Feynman diagrams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Feynman diagrams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feynman_diagrams" title="Feynman diagrams">Feynman diagrams</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beta_Negative_Decay.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Beta_Negative_Decay.svg/220px-Beta_Negative_Decay.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Beta_Negative_Decay.svg/330px-Beta_Negative_Decay.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Beta_Negative_Decay.svg/440px-Beta_Negative_Decay.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beta_Negative_Decay.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Feynman diagram for the decay of a neutron into a proton. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W_boson" title="W boson">W boson</a> is between two vertices indicating a repulsion.</div></div></div>
<p>In modern <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>, forces and the acceleration of particles are explained as a mathematical by-product of exchange of momentum-carrying <a href="/wiki/Gauge_boson" title="Gauge boson">gauge bosons</a>. With the development of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_field_theory" title="Quantum field theory">quantum field theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, it was realized that force is a redundant concept arising from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_momentum" title="Conservation of momentum">conservation of momentum</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4-momentum" title="4-momentum">4-momentum</a> in relativity and momentum of <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">virtual particles</a> in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a>). The conservation of momentum can be directly derived from the homogeneity or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_physics" title="Symmetry in physics">symmetry</a> of <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a> and so is usually considered more fundamental than the concept of a force. Thus the currently known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_forces" title="Fundamental forces">fundamental forces</a> are considered more accurately to be "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_interactions" title="Fundamental interactions">fundamental interactions</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-final_theory_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-final_theory-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 199–128">: 199–128 </span></sup> When particle A emits (creates) or absorbs (annihilates) virtual particle B, a momentum conservation results in recoil of particle A making impression of repulsion or attraction between particles A A' exchanging by B. This description applies to all forces arising from fundamental interactions. While sophisticated mathematical descriptions are needed to predict, in full detail, the accurate result of such interactions, there is a conceptually simple way to describe such interactions through the use of Feynman diagrams. In a Feynman diagram, each matter particle is represented as a straight line (see <a href="/wiki/World_line" title="World line">world line</a>) traveling through time, which normally increases up or to the right in the diagram. Matter and anti-matter particles are identical except for their direction of propagation through the Feynman diagram. World lines of particles intersect at interaction vertices, and the Feynman diagram represents any force arising from an interaction as occurring at the vertex with an associated instantaneous change in the direction of the particle world lines. Gauge bosons are emitted away from the vertex as wavy lines and, in the case of virtual particle exchange, are absorbed at an adjacent vertex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shifman_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shifman-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The utility of Feynman diagrams is that other types of physical phenomena that are part of the general picture of <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental interactions</a> but are conceptually separate from forces can also be described using the same rules. For example, a Feynman diagram can describe in succinct detail how a <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">decays</a> into an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrino</a>, an interaction mediated by the same gauge boson that is responsible for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_nuclear_force" title="Weak nuclear force">weak nuclear force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shifman_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shifman-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fundamental_forces">Fundamental forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Fundamental forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">Fundamental interaction</a></div>
<p>All of the known forces of the universe are classified into four <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental interactions</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_force" title="Strong force">strong</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_force" title="Weak force">weak</a> forces act only at very short distances, and are responsible for the interactions between <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a> and compound <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic force</a> acts between <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charges</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_force" title="Gravitational force">gravitational force</a> acts between <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">masses</a>. All other forces in nature derive from these four fundamental interactions. For example, <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> is a manifestation of the electromagnetic force acting between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atoms" title="Atoms">atoms</a> of two surfaces, and the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup> which does not permit atoms to pass through each other. Similarly, the forces in <a href="/wiki/Spring_(device)" title="Spring (device)">springs</a>, modeled by <a href="/wiki/Hooke%27s_law" title="Hooke's law">Hooke's law</a>, are the result of electromagnetic forces and the Pauli exclusion principle acting together to return an object to its <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_equilibrium" title="Mechanical equilibrium">equilibrium</a> position. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force_(fictitious)" title="Centrifugal force (fictitious)">Centrifugal forces</a> are <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> forces that arise simply from the acceleration of <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotating</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frames_of_reference" title="Frames of reference">frames of reference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 12–11">: 12–11 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 359">: 359 </span></sup>
</p><p>The fundamental theories for forces developed from the <a href="/wiki/Unified_field_theory" title="Unified field theory">unification</a> of different ideas. For example, Sir <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> unified, with his universal theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a>, the force responsible for objects falling near the surface of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> with the force responsible for the falling of celestial bodies about the Earth (the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>) and around the Sun (the planets). <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> demonstrated that electric and magnetic forces were unified through a theory of electromagnetism. In the 20th century, the development of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> led to a modern understanding that the first three fundamental forces (all except gravity) are manifestations of matter (<a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a>) interacting by exchanging <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">virtual particles</a> called <a href="/wiki/Gauge_boson" title="Gauge boson">gauge bosons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of particle physics assumes a similarity between the forces and led scientists to predict the unification of the weak and electromagnetic forces in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electroweak" title="Electroweak">electroweak</a> theory, which was subsequently confirmed by observation. The complete formulation of the Standard Model predicts an as yet unobserved <a href="/wiki/Higgs_mechanism" title="Higgs mechanism">Higgs mechanism</a>, but observations such as <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_oscillation" title="Neutrino oscillation">neutrino oscillations</a> suggest that the Standard Model is incomplete. A <a href="/wiki/Grand_Unified_Theory" title="Grand Unified Theory">Grand Unified Theory</a> that allows for the combination of the electroweak interaction with the strong force is held out as a possibility with candidate theories such as <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetry</a> proposed to accommodate some of the outstanding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="Unsolved problems in physics">unsolved problems in physics</a>. Physicists are still attempting to develop self-consistent unification models that would combine all four fundamental interactions into a <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_everything" title="Theory of everything">theory of everything</a>. Einstein tried and failed at this endeavor, but currently the most popular approach to answering this question is <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-final_theory_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-final_theory-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 212–219">: 212–219 </span></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational">Gravitational</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Gravitational">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">Gravity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Falling_ball.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2751" data-file-width="819" decoding="async" height="571" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Falling_ball.jpg/170px-Falling_ball.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Falling_ball.jpg/255px-Falling_ball.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Falling_ball.jpg/340px-Falling_ball.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Falling_ball.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Images of a freely falling basketball taken with a <a href="/wiki/Stroboscope" title="Stroboscope">stroboscope</a> at 20 flashes per second. The distance units on the right are multiples of about 12 millimeters. The basketball starts at rest.  At the time of the first flash (distance zero) it is released, after which the number of units fallen is equal to the square of the number of flashes.</div></div></div>
<p>What we now call gravity was not identified as a universal force until the work of Isaac Newton. Before Newton, the tendency for objects to fall towards the Earth was not understood to be related to the motions of celestial objects. Galileo was instrumental in describing the characteristics of falling objects by determining that the <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> of every object in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free-fall" title="Free-fall">free-fall</a> was constant and independent of the mass of the object. Today, this <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_acceleration" title="Gravitational acceleration">acceleration due to gravity</a> towards the surface of the Earth is usually designated as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {g}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>g</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\vec {g}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\vec {g}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8aad928c73fda5199478a151663f0ce3a57a8027" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.174ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span> and has a magnitude of about 9.81 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter" title="Meter">meters</a> per second squared (this measurement is taken from sea level and may vary depending on location), and points toward the center of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> This observation means that the force of gravity on an object at the Earth's surface is directly proportional to the object's mass. Thus an object that has a mass of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=m{\vec {g}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/06124270224ceed9300b3f35c2cc9041b1ef49c1" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.084ex; height:3.176ex;"/></a></div>
</p><p>For an object in free-fall, this force is unopposed and the net force on the object is its weight. For objects not in free-fall, the force of gravity is opposed by the reaction forces applied by their supports. For example, a person standing on the ground experiences zero net force, since a <a href="/wiki/Normal_force" title="Normal force">normal force</a> (a reaction force) is exerted by the ground upward on the person that counterbalances his weight that is directed downward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Newton's contribution to gravitational theory was to unify the motions of heavenly bodies, which Aristotle had assumed were in a natural state of constant motion, with falling motion observed on the Earth. He proposed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_gravity" title="Newton's law of gravity">law of gravity</a> that could account for the celestial motions that had been described earlier using <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">Kepler's laws of planetary motion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_ch4_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-uniphysics_ch4-32">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Newton came to realize that the effects of gravity might be observed in different ways at larger distances. In particular, Newton determined that the acceleration of the Moon around the Earth could be ascribed to the same force of gravity if the acceleration due to gravity decreased as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverse_square_law" title="Inverse square law">inverse square law</a>. Further, Newton realized that the acceleration of a body due to gravity is proportional to the mass of the other attracting body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_ch4_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-uniphysics_ch4-32">[29]</a></sup> Combining these ideas gives a formula that relates the mass (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m_{\oplus }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle m_{\oplus }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac952e12b033a89c69c96870bf9cde0f729a074f" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.551ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>) and the radius (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle R_{\oplus }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>where the vector direction is given by <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\hat {r}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>In this equation, a dimensional constant <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle G}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>G</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle G}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="G" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5f3c8921a3b352de45446a6789b104458c9f90b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.827ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is used to describe the relative strength of gravity. This constant has come to be known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_Universal_Gravitation_Constant" title="Newton's Universal Gravitation Constant">Newton's Universal Gravitation Constant</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup> though its value was unknown in Newton's lifetime. Not until 1798 was <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> able to make the first measurement of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle G}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>G</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle G}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="G" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5f3c8921a3b352de45446a6789b104458c9f90b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.827ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torsion_balance" title="Torsion balance">torsion balance</a>; this was widely reported in the press as a measurement of the mass of the Earth since knowing <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle G}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>G</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle G}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="G" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5f3c8921a3b352de45446a6789b104458c9f90b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.827ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> could allow one to solve for the Earth's mass given the above equation. Newton, however, realized that since all celestial bodies followed the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws" title="Kepler's laws">laws of motion</a>, his law of gravity had to be universal. Succinctly stated, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_Law_of_Gravitation" title="Newton's Law of Gravitation">Newton's Law of Gravitation</a> states that the force on a spherical object of mass <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m_{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="m_{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/31aafa60e48d39ccce922404c0b80340b2cc777a" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.095ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> due to the gravitational pull of mass <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m_{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
