where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle r}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mover>

</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\hat {r}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\hat {r}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1009619964ce33a4a02aaa7cf82adc0fb0a50f23" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.292ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the unit vector pointed in the direction away from the center of the first object toward the center of the second object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Principia_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-Principia-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>This formula was powerful enough to stand as the basis for all subsequent descriptions of motion within the solar system until the 20th century. During that time, sophisticated methods of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perturbation_analysis" title="Perturbation analysis">perturbation analysis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup> were invented to calculate the deviations of <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a> due to the influence of multiple bodies on a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a>. The formalism was exact enough to allow mathematicians to predict the existence of the planet <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> before it was observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neptdisc_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neptdisc-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GRAVITY_A_powerful_new_probe_of_black_holes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/GRAVITY_A_powerful_new_probe_of_black_holes.jpg/220px-GRAVITY_A_powerful_new_probe_of_black_holes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/GRAVITY_A_powerful_new_probe_of_black_holes.jpg/330px-GRAVITY_A_powerful_new_probe_of_black_holes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/GRAVITY_A_powerful_new_probe_of_black_holes.jpg/440px-GRAVITY_A_powerful_new_probe_of_black_holes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GRAVITY_A_powerful_new_probe_of_black_holes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Instruments like GRAVITY provide a powerful probe for gravity force detection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>'s orbit, however, did not match that predicted by Newton's Law of Gravitation. Some astrophysicists predicted the existence of another planet (<a href="/wiki/Vulcan_(hypothetical_planet)" title="Vulcan (hypothetical planet)">Vulcan</a>) that would explain the discrepancies; however no such planet could be found. When <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> formulated his theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> (GR) he turned his attention to the problem of Mercury's orbit and found that his theory added <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion_precession_of_Mercury" title="Perihelion precession of Mercury">a correction, which could account for the discrepancy</a>. This was the first time that Newton's Theory of Gravity had been shown to be inexact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since then, general relativity has been acknowledged as the theory that best explains gravity. In GR, gravitation is not viewed as a force, but rather, objects moving freely in gravitational fields travel under their own inertia in <a href="/wiki/Geodesic" title="Geodesic">straight lines</a> through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curved_space-time" title="Curved space-time">curved space-time</a> – defined as the shortest space-time path between two space-time events. From the perspective of the object, all motion occurs as if there were no gravitation whatsoever. It is only when observing the motion in a global sense that the curvature of space-time can be observed and the force is inferred from the object's curved path. Thus, the straight line path in space-time is seen as a curved line in space, and it is called the <i><a href="/wiki/External_ballistics" title="External ballistics">ballistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">trajectory</a></i> of the object. For example, a <a href="/wiki/Basketball_(ball)" title="Basketball (ball)">basketball</a> thrown from the ground moves in a <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a>, as it is in a uniform gravitational field. Its space-time trajectory is almost a straight line, slightly curved (with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radius_of_curvature_(applications)" title="Radius of curvature (applications)">radius of curvature</a> of the order of few <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a>). The time derivative of the changing momentum of the object is what we label as "gravitational force".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetic">Electromagnetic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Electromagnetic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">Electromagnetic force</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_force" title="Electrostatic force">electrostatic force</a> was first described in 1784 by Coulomb as a force that existed intrinsically between two <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">charges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cutnell_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cutnell-22">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 519">: 519 </span></sup> The properties of the electrostatic force were that it varied as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverse_square_law" title="Inverse square law">inverse square law</a> directed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_coordinates" title="Polar coordinates">radial direction</a>, was both attractive and repulsive (there was intrinsic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_polarity" title="Electrical polarity">polarity</a>), was independent of the mass of the charged objects, and followed the <a href="/wiki/Superposition_principle" title="Superposition principle">superposition principle</a>. <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb's law">Coulomb's law</a> unifies all these observations into one succinct statement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulomb_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coulomb-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Subsequent mathematicians and physicists found the construct of the <i><a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a></i> to be useful for determining the electrostatic force on an electric charge at any point in space. The electric field was based on using a hypothetical "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Test_charge" title="Test charge">test charge</a>" anywhere in space and then using Coulomb's Law to determine the electrostatic force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol2_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol2-39">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 4-6 to 4-8">: 4-6 to 4-8 </span></sup> Thus the electric field anywhere in space is defined as

where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/06809d64fa7c817ffc7e323f85997f783dbdf71d" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.07ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is the magnitude of the hypothetical test charge.
</p><p>Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_force" title="Lorentz force">Lorentz force</a> of <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a> was discovered to exist between two <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric currents</a>. It has the same mathematical character as Coulomb's Law with the proviso that like currents attract and unlike currents repel. Similar to the electric field, the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> can be used to determine the magnetic force on an electric current at any point in space. In this case, the magnitude of the magnetic field was determined to be

The magnetic field exerts a force on all <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">magnets</a> including, for example, those used in <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compasses</a>. The fact that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geomagnetism" title="Geomagnetism">Earth's magnetic field</a> is aligned closely with the orientation of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">axis</a> causes compass magnets to become oriented because of the magnetic force pulling on the needle.
</p><p>Through combining the definition of electric current as the time rate of change of electric charge, a rule of <a href="/wiki/Cross_product" title="Cross product">vector multiplication</a> called <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_force" title="Lorentz force">Lorentz's Law</a> describes the force on a charge moving in a magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol2_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol2-39">[36]</a></sup> The connection between electricity and magnetism allows for the description of a unified <i>electromagnetic force</i> that acts on a charge. This force can be written as a sum of the electrostatic force (due to the electric field) and the magnetic force (due to the magnetic field). Fully stated, this is the law:

<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=q\left({\vec {E}}+{\vec {v}}\times {\vec {B}}\right)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=q\left({\vec {E}}+{\vec {v}}\times {\vec {B}}\right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8d0579b4ac1d59c813440ab7b9fa6f459fe469e1" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:19.497ex; height:4.843ex;"/></a></div>
where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mover>

</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\vec {B}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\vec {B}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/83ae7d80cab55b606de217162280b2279142bbb4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.764ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>).
</p><p>The origin of electric and magnetic fields would not be fully explained until 1864 when <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> unified a number of earlier theories into a set of 20 scalar equations, which were later reformulated into 4 vector equations by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Heaviside" title="Oliver Heaviside">Oliver Heaviside</a> and <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Willard_Gibbs" title="Josiah Willard Gibbs">Josiah Willard Gibbs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> These "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maxwell_Equations" title="Maxwell Equations">Maxwell Equations</a>" fully described the sources of the fields as being stationary and moving charges, and the interactions of the fields themselves. This led Maxwell to discover that electric and magnetic fields could be "self-generating" through a <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">wave</a> that traveled at a speed that he calculated to be the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. This insight united the nascent fields of electromagnetic theory with <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a> and led directly to a complete description of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, attempting to reconcile electromagnetic theory with two observations, the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>, and the nonexistence of the <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_catastrophe" title="Ultraviolet catastrophe">ultraviolet catastrophe</a>, proved troublesome. Through the work of leading theoretical physicists, a new theory of electromagnetism was developed using quantum mechanics. This final modification to electromagnetic theory ultimately led to <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a> (or QED), which fully describes all electromagnetic phenomena as being mediated by wave–particles known as <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>. In QED, photons are the fundamental exchange particle, which described all interactions relating to electromagnetism including the electromagnetic force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[Note 4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Strong_nuclear">Strong nuclear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Strong nuclear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">Strong interaction</a></div>
<p>There are two "nuclear forces", which today are usually described as interactions that take place in quantum theories of particle physics. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_nuclear_force" title="Strong nuclear force">strong nuclear force</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cutnell_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cutnell-22">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 940">: 940 </span></sup> is the force responsible for the structural integrity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_nuclei" title="Atomic nuclei">atomic nuclei</a> while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_nuclear_force" title="Weak nuclear force">weak nuclear force</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cutnell_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cutnell-22">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 951">: 951 </span></sup> is responsible for the decay of certain <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a> into <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a> and other types of <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-11"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The strong force is today understood to represent the <a href="/wiki/Interaction" title="Interaction">interactions</a> between <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluons</a> as detailed by the theory of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics" title="Quantum chromodynamics">quantum chromodynamics</a> (QCD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[39]</a></sup> The strong force is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_force" title="Fundamental force">fundamental force</a> mediated by gluons, acting upon quarks, <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">antiquarks</a>, and the gluons themselves. The (aptly named) strong interaction is the "strongest" of the four fundamental forces.
</p><p>The strong force only acts <i>directly</i> upon elementary particles. However, a residual of the force is observed between <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a> (the best known example being the force that acts between <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a> in atomic nuclei) as the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a>. Here the strong force acts indirectly, transmitted as gluons, which form part of the virtual pi and rho <a href="/wiki/Meson" title="Meson">mesons</a>, which classically transmit the nuclear force (see this topic for more). The failure of many searches for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_quark" title="Free quark">free quarks</a> has shown that the elementary particles affected are not directly observable. This phenomenon is called <a href="/wiki/Color_confinement" title="Color confinement">color confinement</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weak_nuclear">Weak nuclear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Weak nuclear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">Weak interaction</a></div>
<p>The weak force is due to the exchange of the heavy <a href="/wiki/W_and_Z_bosons" title="W and Z bosons">W and Z bosons</a>. Since the weak force is mediated by two types of bosons, it can be divided into two types of interaction or "<a href="/wiki/Feynman_diagram" title="Feynman diagram">vertices</a>" — <a href="/wiki/Charged_current" title="Charged current">charged current</a>, involving the electrically charged W<sup>+</sup> and W<sup>−</sup> bosons, and <a href="/wiki/Neutral_current" title="Neutral current">neutral current</a>, involving electrically neutral Z<sup>0</sup> bosons. The most familiar effect of weak interaction is <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> (of neutrons in atomic nuclei) and the associated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a>. This is a type of charged-current interaction. The word "weak" derives from the fact that the field strength is some 10<sup>13</sup> times less than that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_force" title="Strong force">strong force</a>. Still, it is stronger than gravity over short distances. A consistent electroweak theory has also been developed, which shows that electromagnetic forces and the weak force are indistinguishable at a temperatures in excess of approximately 10<sup>15</sup> <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvins</a>. Such temperatures have been probed in modern <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a> and show the conditions of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> in the early moments of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-fundamental_forces">Non-fundamental forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Non-fundamental forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some forces are consequences of the fundamental ones. In such situations, idealized models can be utilized to gain physical insight.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Normal_force">Normal force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Normal force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Incline.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="262" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Incline.svg/220px-Incline.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Incline.svg/330px-Incline.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Incline.svg/440px-Incline.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Incline.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>F<sub>N</sub></i> represents the <a href="/wiki/Normal_force" title="Normal force">normal force</a> exerted on the object.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Normal_force" title="Normal force">Normal force</a></div>
<p>The normal force is due to repulsive forces of interaction between atoms at close contact. When their electron clouds overlap, Pauli repulsion (due to <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermionic</a> nature of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>) follows resulting in the force that acts in a direction <a href="/wiki/Normal_(geometry)" title="Normal (geometry)">normal</a> to the surface interface between two objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cutnell_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-Cutnell-22">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 93">: 93 </span></sup> The normal force, for example, is responsible for the structural integrity of tables and floors as well as being the force that responds whenever an external force pushes on a solid object. An example of the normal force in action is the impact force on an object crashing into an immobile surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-12"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Friction">Friction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Friction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">Friction</a></div>
<p>Friction is a surface force that opposes relative motion. The frictional force is directly related to the normal force that acts to keep two solid objects separated at the point of contact. There are two broad classifications of frictional forces: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Static_friction" title="Static friction">static friction</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinetic_friction" title="Kinetic friction">kinetic friction</a>.
</p><p>The static friction force (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F_{\mathrm {sf} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>The kinetic friction force (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F_{\mathrm {kf} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mstyle>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F_{\mathrm {kf} }=\mu _{\mathrm {kf} }F_{\mathrm {N} },}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle F_{\mathrm {kf} }=\mu _{\mathrm {kf} }F_{\mathrm {N} },}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dbaae10d1a2126c29fd2301cadab441ce7143b42" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:13.025ex; height:2.676ex;"/></div>
</p><p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mu _{\mathrm {kf} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">f</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mu _{\mathrm {kf} }}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mu _{\mathrm {kf} }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/694b74bd5a41a7b736b6427ebbc1bb01c912761c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.114ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_kinetic_friction" title="Coefficient of kinetic friction">coefficient of kinetic friction</a>. For most surface interfaces, the coefficient of kinetic friction is less than the coefficient of static friction.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tension">Tension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Tension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tension_(physics)" title="Tension (physics)">Tension (physics)</a></div>
<p>Tension forces can be modeled using <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ideal_string&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ideal string (page does not exist)">ideal strings</a> that are massless, frictionless, unbreakable, and unstretchable. They can be combined with ideal <a href="/wiki/Pulley" title="Pulley">pulleys</a>, which allow ideal strings to switch physical direction. Ideal strings transmit tension forces instantaneously in action-reaction pairs so that if two objects are connected by an ideal string, any force directed along the string by the first object is accompanied by a force directed along the string in the opposite direction by the second object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[40]</a></sup> By connecting the same string multiple times to the same object through the use of a set-up that uses movable pulleys, the tension force on a load can be multiplied. For every string that acts on a load, another factor of the tension force in the string acts on the load. However, even though such machines allow for an <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_advantage" title="Mechanical advantage">increase in force</a>, there is a corresponding increase in the length of string that must be displaced in order to move the load. These tandem effects result ultimately in the <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">conservation of mechanical energy</a> since the <a href="#Kinematic_integrals">work done on the load</a> is the same no matter how complicated the machine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-13"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-13"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elastic_force">Elastic force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Elastic force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">Elasticity (physics)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hooke%27s_law" title="Hooke's law">Hooke's law</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mass-spring-system.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1699" data-file-width="1030" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Mass-spring-system.png/170px-Mass-spring-system.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Mass-spring-system.png/255px-Mass-spring-system.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Mass-spring-system.png/340px-Mass-spring-system.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mass-spring-system.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>F<sub>k</sub></i> is the force that responds to the load on the spring</div></div></div>
<p>An elastic force acts to return a <a href="/wiki/Spring_(device)" title="Spring (device)">spring</a> to its natural length. An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ideal_spring" title="Ideal spring">ideal spring</a> is taken to be massless, frictionless, unbreakable, and infinitely stretchable. Such springs exert forces that push when contracted, or pull when extended, in proportion to the <a href="/wiki/Displacement_field_(mechanics)" title="Displacement field (mechanics)">displacement</a> of the spring from its equilibrium position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[42]</a></sup> This linear relationship was described by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> in 1676, for whom <a href="/wiki/Hooke%27s_law" title="Hooke's law">Hooke's law</a> is named. If <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Delta x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f3890eb866b6258d7a304fc34c70ee3fb3a81a70" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.266ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the displacement, the force exerted by an ideal spring equals:

where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle k}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

The minus sign accounts for the tendency of the force to act in opposition to the applied load.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-14"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-14"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continuum_mechanics">Continuum mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Continuum mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stokes_sphere.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="403" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Stokes_sphere.svg/170px-Stokes_sphere.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Stokes_sphere.svg/255px-Stokes_sphere.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Stokes_sphere.svg/340px-Stokes_sphere.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stokes_sphere.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>When the drag force (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F_{d}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>

<semantics>

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F_{g}}</annotation>

</math></span><img alt="F_{g}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/03cf1bda064b272b4a9aff2baab298c67c3d7a38" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.516ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>), the object reaches a state of <a href="#Dynamic_equilibrium">dynamic equilibrium</a> at <a href="/wiki/Terminal_velocity" title="Terminal velocity">terminal velocity</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">Pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">Drag (physics)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">Stress (mechanics)</a></div>
<p>Newton's laws and Newtonian mechanics in general were first developed to describe how forces affect idealized <a href="/wiki/Point_particle" title="Point particle">point particles</a> rather than three-dimensional objects. However, in real life, matter has extended structure and forces that act on one part of an object might affect other parts of an object. For situations where lattice holding together the atoms in an object is able to flow, contract, expand, or otherwise change shape, the theories of <a href="/wiki/Continuum_mechanics" title="Continuum mechanics">continuum mechanics</a> describe the way forces affect the material. For example, in extended <a href="/wiki/Fluid_mechanics" title="Fluid mechanics">fluids</a>, differences in <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> result in forces being directed along the pressure <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">gradients</a> as follows:

</p><p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle V}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>

Pressure gradients and differentials result in the <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyant force</a> for fluids suspended in gravitational fields, winds in <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_science" title="Atmospheric science">atmospheric science</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lift_(physics)" title="Lift (physics)">lift</a> associated with <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamics" title="Aerodynamics">aerodynamics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flight" title="Flight">flight</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-15"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-15"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>A specific instance of such a force that is associated with <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_pressure" title="Dynamic pressure">dynamic pressure</a> is fluid resistance: a body force that resists the motion of an object through a fluid due to <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>. For so-called "<a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)#Very_low_Reynolds_numbers_–_Stokes'_drag" title="Drag (physics)">Stokes' drag</a>" the force is approximately proportional to the velocity, but opposite in direction:

<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {v}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>More formally, forces in <a href="/wiki/Continuum_mechanics" title="Continuum mechanics">continuum mechanics</a> are fully described by a <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">stress</a>–<a href="/wiki/Tensor" title="Tensor">tensor</a> with terms that are roughly defined as

</p><p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle A}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>A</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="A" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7daff47fa58cdfd29dc333def748ff5fa4c923e3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.743ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the relevant cross-sectional area for the volume for which the stress-tensor is being calculated. This formalism includes pressure terms associated with forces that act normal to the cross-sectional area (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrix_diagonal" title="Matrix diagonal">matrix diagonals</a> of the tensor) as well as <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">shear</a> terms associated with forces that act <a href="/wiki/Parallel_(geometry)" title="Parallel (geometry)">parallel</a> to the cross-sectional area (the off-diagonal elements). The stress tensor accounts for forces that cause all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strain_(physics)" title="Strain (physics)">strains</a> (deformations) including also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_stress" title="Tensile stress">tensile stresses</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compression_(physical)" title="Compression (physical)">compressions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uniphysics_ch2_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-uniphysics_ch2-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-17"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 133–134">: 133–134 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol2_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol2-39">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 38-1–38-11">: 38-1–38-11 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fictitious_forces">Fictitious forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Fictitious forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fictitious_forces" title="Fictitious forces">Fictitious forces</a></div>
<p>There are forces that are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frame_dependent" title="Frame dependent">frame dependent</a>, meaning that they appear due to the adoption of non-Newtonian (that is, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-inertial_frame" title="Non-inertial frame">non-inertial</a>) <a href="/wiki/Frame_of_reference" title="Frame of reference">reference frames</a>. Such forces include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centrifugal_force_(rotating_reference_frame)" title="Centrifugal force (rotating reference frame)">centrifugal force</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_force" title="Coriolis force">Coriolis force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup> These forces are considered fictitious because they do not exist in frames of reference that are not accelerating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-17"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-18"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup> Because these forces are not genuine they are also referred to as "pseudo forces".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-18"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 12–11">: 12–11 </span></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> becomes a fictitious force that arises in situations where spacetime deviates from a flat geometry. As an extension, <a href="/wiki/Kaluza%E2%80%93Klein_theory" title="Kaluza–Klein theory">Kaluza–Klein theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a> ascribe electromagnetism and the other <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental forces</a> respectively to the curvature of differently scaled dimensions, which would ultimately imply that all forces are fictitious.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotations_and_torque">Rotations and torque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Rotations and torque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Torque_animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="154" data-file-width="220" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/09/Torque_animation.gif" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption">Relationship between force (F), torque (τ), and <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">momentum</a> vectors (p and L) in a rotating system.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">Torque</a></div>
<p>Forces that cause extended objects to rotate are associated with <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torques</a>. Mathematically, the torque of a force <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {r}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>Torque is the rotation equivalent of force in the same way that <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">angle</a> is the rotational equivalent for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Position_(vector)" title="Position (vector)">position</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">angular velocity</a> for <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a> for <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a>. As a consequence of Newton's First Law of Motion, there exists <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotational_inertia" title="Rotational inertia">rotational inertia</a> that ensures that all bodies maintain their angular momentum unless acted upon by an unbalanced torque. Likewise, Newton's Second Law of Motion can be used to derive an analogous equation for the instantaneous <a href="/wiki/Angular_acceleration" title="Angular acceleration">angular acceleration</a> of the rigid body:

</p><p>where

<ul><li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle I}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {\alpha }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
</mstyle>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\vec {\alpha }}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\vec {\alpha }}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/74b66e3c1118363d17c9e55e1858c8e402b25c70" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.488ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> is the angular acceleration of the body.</li></ul>
<p>This provides a definition for the moment of inertia, which is the rotational equivalent for mass. In more advanced treatments of mechanics, where the rotation over a time interval is described, the moment of inertia must be substituted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moment_of_inertia_tensor" title="Moment of inertia tensor">tensor</a> that, when properly analyzed, fully determines the characteristics of rotations including <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">precession</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nutation" title="Nutation">nutation</a>.
</p><p>Equivalently, the differential form of Newton's Second Law provides an alternative definition of torque:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup>

</p><p>Newton's Third Law of Motion requires that all objects exerting torques themselves experience equal and opposite torques,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[45]</a></sup> and therefore also directly implies the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">conservation of angular momentum</a> for closed systems that experience rotations and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolution_(geometry)" title="Revolution (geometry)">revolutions</a> through the action of internal torques.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Centripetal_force">Centripetal force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Centripetal force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Centripetal_force" title="Centripetal force">Centripetal force</a></div>
<p>For an object accelerating in circular motion, the unbalanced force acting on the object equals:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup>

</p><p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\hat {r}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\hat {r}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1009619964ce33a4a02aaa7cf82adc0fb0a50f23" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.292ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Unit_vector" title="Unit vector">unit vector</a> pointing in the radial direction outwards from the center. This means that the unbalanced centripetal force felt by any object is always directed toward the center of the curving path. Such forces act perpendicular to the velocity vector associated with the motion of an object, and therefore do not change the <a href="/wiki/Speed" title="Speed">speed</a> of the object (magnitude of the velocity), but only the direction of the velocity vector. The unbalanced force that accelerates an object can be resolved into a component that is perpendicular to the path, and one that is tangential to the path. This yields both the tangential force, which accelerates the object by either slowing it down or speeding it up, and the radial (centripetal) force, which changes its direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-19"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-19"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinematic_integrals">Kinematic integrals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Kinematic integrals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Impulse_(physics)" title="Impulse (physics)">Impulse</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_work" title="Mechanical work">Mechanical work</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">Power (physics)</a></div>
<p>Forces can be used to define a number of physical concepts by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integration_(calculus)" title="Integration (calculus)">integrating</a> with respect to <a href="/wiki/Kinematics" title="Kinematics">kinematic variables</a>. For example, integrating with respect to time gives the definition of <a href="/wiki/Impulse_(physics)" title="Impulse (physics)">impulse</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[47]</a></sup>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_energy">Potential energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Potential energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">Potential energy</a></div>
<p>Instead of a force, often the mathematically related concept of a <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> field can be used for convenience. For instance, the gravitational force acting upon an object can be seen as the action of the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">gravitational field</a> that is present at the object's location. Restating mathematically the definition of energy (via the definition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_work" title="Mechanical work">work</a>), a potential <a href="/wiki/Scalar_field" title="Scalar field">scalar field</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle U({\vec {r}})}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>U</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>r</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle U({\vec {r}})}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle U({\vec {r}})}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8dec6601005dfdc7b499504dc919d362a3f1b709" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.815ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is defined as that field whose <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">gradient</a> is equal and opposite to the force produced at every point:
<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=-{\vec {\nabla }}U.}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>F</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∇<!-- ∇ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mi>U</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=-{\vec {\nabla }}U.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle {\vec {F}}=-{\vec {\nabla }}U.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/276d2854bc4206784024fa21b5141165f78e1388" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:11.043ex; height:3.009ex;"/></div>
</p><p>Forces can be classified as <a href="/wiki/Conservative_force" title="Conservative force">conservative</a> or nonconservative. Conservative forces are equivalent to the gradient of a <a href="/wiki/Potential" title="Potential">potential</a> while nonconservative forces are not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-23"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-20"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservative_forces">Conservative forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Conservative forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservative_force" title="Conservative force">Conservative force</a></div>
<p>A conservative force that acts on a <a href="/wiki/Closed_system" title="Closed system">closed system</a> has an associated mechanical work that allows energy to convert only between <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential</a> forms. This means that for a closed system, the net <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_energy" title="Mechanical energy">mechanical energy</a> is conserved whenever a conservative force acts on the system. The force, therefore, is related directly to the difference in potential energy between two different locations in space,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup> and can be considered to be an artifact of the potential field in the same way that the direction and amount of a flow of water can be considered to be an artifact of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contour_map" title="Contour map">contour map</a> of the elevation of an area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-24"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-21"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conservative forces include <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetic</a> force, and the <a href="/wiki/Hooke%27s_law" title="Hooke's law">spring</a> force. Each of these forces has models that are dependent on a position often given as a <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radial vector</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {r}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nonconservative_forces">Nonconservative forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Nonconservative forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For certain physical scenarios, it is impossible to model forces as being due to gradient of potentials. This is often due to macrophysical considerations that yield forces as arising from a macroscopic statistical average of <a href="/wiki/Microstate_(statistical_mechanics)" title="Microstate (statistical mechanics)">microstates</a>. For example, friction is caused by the gradients of numerous electrostatic potentials between the <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>, but manifests as a force model that is independent of any macroscale position vector. Nonconservative forces other than friction include other <a href="/wiki/Contact_force" title="Contact force">contact forces</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tension_(physics)" title="Tension (physics)">tension</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_compression" title="Physical compression">compression</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a>. However, for any sufficiently detailed description, all these forces are the results of conservative ones since each of these macroscopic forces are the net results of the gradients of microscopic potentials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-26"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-23"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The connection between macroscopic nonconservative forces and microscopic conservative forces is described by detailed treatment with <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">statistical mechanics</a>. In macroscopic closed systems, nonconservative forces act to change the <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energies</a> of the system, and are often associated with the transfer of heat. According to the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">Second law of thermodynamics</a>, nonconservative forces necessarily result in energy transformations within closed systems from ordered to more random conditions as <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> increases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeynmanVol1_3-27"><a href="#cite_note-FeynmanVol1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_4-24"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Units_of_measurement">Units of measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Units of measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI" title="SI">SI</a> unit of force is the <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">newton</a> (symbol N), which is the force required to accelerate a one kilogram mass at a rate of one meter per second squared, or <span class="texhtml">kg·m·s<sup>−2</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metric_units_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-metric_units-54">[50]</a></sup> The corresponding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGS" title="CGS">CGS</a> unit is the <a href="/wiki/Dyne" title="Dyne">dyne</a>, the force required to accelerate a one gram mass by one centimeter per second squared, or <span class="texhtml">g·cm·s<sup>−2</sup></span>. A newton is thus equal to 100,000 dynes.
</p><p>The gravitational <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foot-pound-second" title="Foot-pound-second">foot-pound-second</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_unit" title="English unit">English unit</a> of force is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound-force" title="Pound-force">pound-force</a> (lbf), defined as the force exerted by gravity on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound-mass" title="Pound-mass">pound-mass</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravity" title="Standard gravity">standard gravitational</a> field of <span class="texhtml">9.80665 m·s<sup>−2</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metric_units_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-metric_units-54">[50]</a></sup> The pound-force provides an alternative unit of mass: one <a href="/wiki/Slug_(unit)" title="Slug (unit)">slug</a> is the mass that will accelerate by one foot per second squared when acted on by one pound-force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metric_units_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-metric_units-54">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative unit of force in a different foot-pound-second system, the absolute fps system, is the <a href="/wiki/Poundal" title="Poundal">poundal</a>, defined as the force required to accelerate a one-pound mass at a rate of one foot per second squared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metric_units_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-metric_units-54">[50]</a></sup> The units of <a href="/wiki/Slug_(unit)" title="Slug (unit)">slug</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poundal" title="Poundal">poundal</a> are designed to avoid a constant of proportionality in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_Second_Law" title="Newton's Second Law">Newton's Second Law</a>.
</p><p>The pound-force has a metric counterpart, less commonly used than the newton: the <a href="/wiki/Kilogram-force" title="Kilogram-force">kilogram-force</a> (kgf) (sometimes kilopond), is the force exerted by standard gravity on one kilogram of mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metric_units_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-metric_units-54">[50]</a></sup> The kilogram-force leads to an alternate, but rarely used unit of mass: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_slug" title="Metric slug">metric slug</a> (sometimes mug or hyl) is that mass that accelerates at <span class="texhtml">1 m·s<sup>−2</sup></span> when subjected to a force of 1 kgf. The kilogram-force is not a part of the modern SI system, and is generally deprecated; however it still sees use for some purposes as expressing aircraft weight, jet thrust, bicycle spoke tension, torque wrench settings and engine output torque. Other arcane units of force include the <a href="/wiki/Sth%C3%A8ne" title="Sthène">sthène</a>, which is equivalent to 1000 N, and the <a href="/wiki/Kip_(unit)" title="Kip (unit)">kip</a>, which is equivalent to 1000 lbf.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Newton's <i>Principia Mathematica</i> actually used a finite difference version of this equation based upon <i>impulse</i>. See <i><a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion#Impulse" title="Newton's laws of motion">Impulse</a></i>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Halliday-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Halliday_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"It is important to note that we <i>cannot</i> derive a general expression for Newton's second law for variable mass systems by treating the mass in <b>F</b> = <i>d</i><b>P</b>/<i>dt</i> = <i>d</i>(<i>M</i><b>v</b>) as a <i>variable</i>. [...] We <i>can</i> use <b>F</b> = <i>d</i><b>P</b>/<i>dt</i> to analyze variable mass systems <i>only</i> if we apply it to an entire system of constant mass having parts among which there is an interchange of mass." [Emphasis as in the original] (<a href="#CITEREFHallidayResnickKrane2001">Halliday, Resnick &amp; Krane 2001</a>, p. 199)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Any single force is only one aspect of a mutual interaction between <i>two</i> bodies." (<a href="#CITEREFHallidayResnickKrane2001">Halliday, Resnick &amp; Krane 2001</a>, pp. 78–79)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a complete library on quantum mechanics see <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics#References" title="Quantum mechanics">Quantum mechanics – References</a></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFKibbleBerkshire2004">Kibble, Tom W.B.; Berkshire, Frank H. (2004). <i>Classical Mechanics</i> (Fifth ed.). According to page 12, "[Force] can of course be introduced, by defining it through Newton's second law".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFde_LangePierrus2010">de Lange, O. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox">
